Meow
Colin, I love your videos but I cannot say this strongly enough, NEVER get in an unsupported excavation!!! People have died doing this. In civil engineering the practice is a sackable offence. While clay soils stand vertically temporarily, excavations in them can collapse catastrophically without any warning. Ask anyone with an understanding of geotechnics to understand why.   I know you eventually supported the dig but there were clear shots of you in the excavation with no support. Excavations should be supported at all times if someone is in them. Miners use to use moveable walls of brick that they dug below so they were always protected, there are modern equivalents of this.   I hope none of your future plans for this project involve anymore unsupported digging. If so, please reconsider your plans and get in contact with someone who understands geotechnics/engineering geology.

Its very exciting to see a tunnel being dug. You should however be well informed of the dangers involved with tunneling. Your soil looks pretty cohesive; clay and rocks, not to bad a ground. No groundwater to be seen either. So that is also a big plus.   You want to support the ground every 3 feet or so with some big sturdy beams. So it wont be coming down on you. If you make the tunnel a bit bigger, you could use some molds and make the tunnel out of concrete with some rebar in it. Or use steel like you did with your bunker, and maybe use some grout (thin  concrete) to pump behind the steel tunnel liner, so everything is nice and snug (safe). You could also do that with your shaft you dug already!   Greetings,   Jeff

Geotechnical Professional here. I know safety might not be your major concern, but what you are doing has some serious risk of instant death to it. You seem to have considered collapse and the soil and bedrock you've discovered seems to be stable. That said, please please please either monitor the atmosphere or install ventilation. If you start running equipment at depth, you may displace the oxygen and just fall asleep (followed by death). This is a very cool project, I would hate to see you die for it. (P.S. there are many other concerns for you to take into consideration. Please at least talk with people who know about tunneling to help you mitigate the very real possibility of instant death.)

The Great Inscape  (IN the garden)
When I was digging a new pit for the mulching stuff (I bury all vegetable matter from garden & Kitchen, which saves on garbage and is healthier for the enviroment) I used my old pole holer,  which is basically a steel tube attached to a steel handle, with bent over angled blades on the bottom end.  This you screw into the ground and it basically cuts into the soil and forces the soil up the pipe, and you then just up end the pole holer every now and then and pour the loose soil out onto your pile (or in your case into the bucket).  Eventually you've got lots of holes to just break away instead of one solid mass.  Makes it easier to dig a pit, especially in the root system that pervades my foresty land.    It strikes me that a hydraulic version of this idea with a giant vacuum cleaner style pump to suck the soil and debris out of the holer would be a workable solution for digging both down and sideways to make the side tunnel.   Its a thought anyway.

Abrasive tools would be fairly good for low noise. As in, they grind away the rock instead of pounding it away. An angle grinder is though a bit fast and makes a lot of noise from its gears alone... But the noise it makes from cutting it actually fairly small since it has no real impacts during operation. And the neighbors are likely used to the sound of the angle grinder to be fair.  How well grinding scores into the rock to extract bits is, or how fast such digging is, I have no clue. But if you put up some sound deadening material on the walls, then it would likely be fairly quiet outside of the shed.

Hey Colin, I did too much digging in a crawl space in clay soil (like you have there). I found a little baby shovel (about 1 meter long) at the hardware store worked great in the confined space. The difference from your short shovel is you had a flat blade shovel, which is impossible to use in clay. Switch to a pointy "spade" shape and it's easier. Also, there's a 2 meter long solid steel pole with a wedge on the end that worked great. I bought both tools at Menards (a big home improvement store chain) in the US. The baby shovel didn't have a special name, but the heavy bar (the weight/momentum helps it penetrate the clay ... 16 to 18 lbs, or 8 kg) is called a  "post hole digging bar" or "pinch point bar". Both tools could help you going forward, if you can obtain them in the UK. Good luck! Looking forward to seeing how you tackle the challenges!

That said... the fact the hinges aren't embedded in the metal, thus causing height differences in the carpet - just irks me. Anyway. Cool project, keep'em comin and greetings from Latvia. Edited: also, why aren't there concrete lining being poured in on the walls of the shaft? Weld some threaded bars on to the metal frame that's already there, measure HDF or other plate material (thick veneer, DSP etc) and drill holes in it, assemble the inner formwork out of it (cover the threaded rod holes with large washers and sealing foam) and fix it in place with nuts, seal any cracks with construction foam, pour in the concrete, wait for 10 days (water the concrete daily in the meantime), screw off the nuts, remove the formwork, and voila - the risk the tunnel will start shedding inner skin with time (ie, lose material from walls/sink the sides in, etc, etc) is eliminated, plus everything looking much neater and more "pro". There you go, no need to thank me.
